<p  align="center">
<font size="2" face="Times New Roman"><b>Shoe Carnival, Inc.<br> Evansville, Indiana</b></font></p>
<p  align="center">
<font size="2" face="Times New Roman">Annual Report to Securities and Exchange Commission<br> January 29, 2005</font></p>
<p  align="center"><a name="sc_01"></a>
<font size="2" face="Times New Roman"><b>PART I</b></font></p>
<table align="center"  border="0" style="border-collapse:collapse" cellspacing="0"  cellpadding="0"  width="100%">

 <tr>
  <td width="10%" valign="top">
  <p>
<a name="sc_02"></a><font size="2" face="Times New Roman">ITEM 1.</font></p>  </td>
  <td width="90%" valign="top">
  <p>
<font size="2" face="Times New Roman"><b>BUSINESS</b></font></p>  </td>
 </tr>
</table>



<p><font size="2" face="Times New Roman"><b>Forward-Looking Statements</b></font></p>
<p>
<font size="2" face="Times New Roman">This Annual Report contains forward-looking statements that involve a number of risks and uncertainties.&#160; A number of factors could cause our actual results, performance, achievements or industry results to be materially different from any future results, performance or achievements expressed or implied by these forward-looking statements.&#160; These factors include, but are not limited to: general economic conditions in the areas of the United States in which our stores are located; changes in the overall retail environment and more specifically in the apparel and footwear retail sectors; the potential impact of national and international security concerns on the retail environment; changes in our relationships with key suppliers; the impact of competition and pricing; changes in weather patterns, consumer buying trends and our ability to identify and respond to emerging fashion trends; risks associated with the seasonality of the retail industry; the
availability of desirable store locations at acceptable lease terms and our ability to open new stores in a timely and profitable manner; higher than anticipated costs associated with the closing of underperforming stores; the inability of manufacturers to deliver products in a timely manner; changes in the political and economic environments in the People&#146;s Republic of China, a major manufacturer of footwear; and the continued favorable trade relations between the United States and China and other countries which are the major manufacturers of footwear.&#160; See &#147;BUSINESS - Risk Factors&#148;</font></p>

<p><font size="2" face="Times New Roman"><b>General&#160; </b></font></p>
<p>
<font size="2" face="Times New Roman">Shoe Carnival, Inc. is one of the nation&#146;s largest family footwear retailers.&#160; We offer customers a broad assortment of moderately priced dress, casual and athletic footwear for men, women and children with emphasis on national and regional name brands.&#160; We differentiate ourselves from our competitors by our distinctive, highly promotional in-store marketing effort and large stores that average 11,600 square feet, generate an average of approximately $2.4&nbsp;million in annual sales and house an average inventory of approximately 29,600 pairs of shoes per location.&#160; As of January 29, 2005, we operated 255 stores in 24 states in the Midwest, South and Southeast regions of the United States.</font></p>
<p>
<font size="2" face="Times New Roman">We make available free of charge through the Investor Relations portion of our Internet website at www.shoecarnival.com our annual reports on Form 10-K, our quarterly reports on Form 10-Q, our current reports on Form 8-K and amendments to those reports filed or furnished pursuant to Section 13(a) or Section 15(d) of the Securities Exchange Act of 1934, as amended, as soon as reasonably practicable after we electronically file such material with, or furnish it to, the Securities and Exchange Commission.</font></p>

<p>
<font size="2" face="Times New Roman"><b>Our annual report on Form 10-K as filed with the Securities and Exchange Commission is available without charge to shareholders,
investment professionals and securities analysts upon written request. Requests should be directed to Investor Relations at the corporate address.</b></font></p>

<p>
<font size="2" face="Times New Roman">We are an Indiana corporation that was initially formed in Delaware in 1993 and reincorporated in Indiana in 1996. </font></p>
<p>
<font size="2" face="Times New Roman"><b>Business Strategy</b></font></p>
<p>
<font size="2" face="Times New Roman">Our goal is to continue to grow our net sales and earnings by strengthening our position as the logical destination store for our customers&#146; footwear needs.&#160; Key elements of our business strategy are as follows:</font></p>
<p>
<font size="2" face="Times New Roman"><b><i>We offer a distinctive shopping experience.</i></b>&#160; Our stores combine competitive pricing with a highly promotional, in-store marketing effort that encourages customer participation and creates a fun and exciting shopping experience.&#160; We promote a high-energy retail environment by decorating with bright lights and bold colors, and by featuring a stage and barker as the focal point in each store.&#160; With a microphone, this barker, or &#147;mic-person&#148;, advertises current specials, organizes contests and games, and assists and educates customers with the features and location of merchandise.&#160; Our mic-person offers limited-duration promotions throughout the day, encouraging the customers to take immediate advantage of our value pricing.&#160; We believe this highly promotional atmosphere results in various competitive advantages, including increased multiple unit sales; the building of a loyal, repeat customer base; the creation of
word-of-mouth advertising; and enhanced sell through of in-season goods.</font></p>

<p  align="center"><font size="2" face="Times New Roman">1</font></p>



<div style="page-break-before:always"></div>
<PAGE>
<br>



<p>
<font size="2" face="Times New Roman"><b><i>We offer a broad merchandise assortment.</i></b>&#160;Our objective is to be the destination store-of-choice for a wide range of consumers seeking moderately priced, current season name brand and private label footwear.&#160; Our product assortment includes dress and casual shoes, sandals, boots and a wide assortment of athletic shoes for the entire family.&#160; The average store carries approximately 29,600&nbsp;pairs of shoes in four general categories&nbsp;-&nbsp;men&#146;s, women&#146;s, children&#146;s and athletics.&#160; In addition to footwear, our stores carry selected accessory items complementary to the sale of footwear.&#160; We place significant emphasis on visual merchandising and the promotion of nationally recognized name brands.&#160; We communicate the importance of these brands through creative signage and other visual aids on the fixtures throughout the stores.</font></p>
<p>
<font size="2" face="Times New Roman">We believe that by offering a wide selection of both athletic and non-athletic footwear, we are able to reduce our exposure to shifts in fashion preferences between those categories.&#160; Our ability to identify and react to fashion changes is a key factor in our sales and earnings performance.</font></p>
<p>
<font size="2" face="Times New Roman"><b><i>We offer value to our customers.</i></b>&#160; Our marketing effort targets middle income, value-conscious consumers seeking name brand footwear for all age groups.&#160; We believe that by offering a wide selection of popular styles of name brand merchandise at competitive prices, we generate broad customer appeal.&#160; Additionally, the time-conscious customer appreciates the convenience of one-stop shopping for the entire family.&#160; We also believe our highly promotional in-store shopping environment contributes to a reputation of value pricing throughout the store.</font></p>
<p>
<font size="2" face="Times New Roman"><b><i>We maintain an efficient store level cost structure.</i></b>&#160; Our cost-efficient store operations and real estate strategy enable us to price products competitively and earn attractive store level returns.&#160; Low labor costs are achieved by housing merchandise directly on the selling floor in an open-stock format, enabling customers who choose to serve themselves.&#160; This reduces the staffing required to assist customers and reduces store level labor costs as a percentage of sales.&#160; We prefer to locate stores predominantly in strip shopping centers in order to take advantage of lower occupancy costs and maximize our exposure to value-oriented shoppers.</font></p>

<p><font size="2" face="Times New Roman"><b><i>We rely heavily on information technology</i></b><i>.&#160; </i>We have invested significant resources in information technology.&#160; Our proprietary inventory management and point-of-sale systems provide corporate management, buyers and store managers with the timely information necessary to monitor and control all phases of operations.&#160; Our store managers are able to monitor sales and gross profit margins on a real-time basis throughout the day.&#160; Reacting to sales trends, our mic-people use this information to choose from among a number of product promotions supplied by our centralized merchandising staff.&#160; Our data warehouse enables the buying staff to analyze sales, margin and inventory levels by store, by day, down to the size of shoe if necessary.&#160; Using this information, our merchandise managers meet regularly with vendors to compare their product sales, gross margins and return on inventory investment against previously
stated objectives.&#160; We believe timely access to key business data has enabled us in the past to drive annual comparable store sales increases, manage our markdown activity and improve inventory turnover.</font></p>
<p>
<font size="2" face="Times New Roman"><b>Growth Strategy</b></font></p>
<p>
<font size="2" face="Times New Roman">Key elements of our growth strategy are as follows:</font></p>
<p>
<font size="2" face="Times New Roman"><b><i>We will to continue to grow our store base.</i></b>&#160; Aside from comparable store sales increases, the majority of our sales and earnings growth is expected to be generated by the opening of new stores.&#160; In 2005, we expect to open between 8 and 10 stores, net of store closings. These new stores will be located in large and small markets within our existing geographic areas.&#160; Our intention is to fill in certain under-penetrated larger markets with additional stores, thereby increasing the performance of the overall market.&#160; We also intend to enter smaller markets that we can fully penetrate with one or two stores.&#160; We generally can advertise more effectively in these markets, which helps to create immediate brand awareness.&#160; We have, and will continue to adjust our annual store growth rate based on our view of internal and external opportunities and challenges. &#160;In 2006, we expect to continue to have moderate store growth by
opening between 10 and 20 stores, net of closings.&#160; Beyond 2006, if the retail environment has improved and sufficient real estate sites are available, we expect to accelerate net new store openings to between 25 and 35 stores per year.</font></p>

<p  align="center"><font size="2" face="Times New Roman">2</font></p>



<div style="page-break-before:always"></div>
<PAGE>
<br>



<p>
<font size="2" face="Times New Roman">We typically enter larger markets (populations greater than 400,000) by opening two or more stores at approximately the same time.&#160; In smaller markets that can only support a single store, we generally will seek locations in reasonably close proximity to other existing markets.&#160; This strategy supports more efficient management and reduces distribution costs.&#160; We believe the advantages of clustering stores in existing markets will lead to cost efficiencies and overall incremental sales gains that should more than offset any adverse effect on sales of existing stores.</font></p>
<p>
<font size="2" face="Times New Roman"><b><i>One of our major goals is to improve our operating margins.</i></b> We are focused on improving our operating margins by increasing our gross margin and leveraging general and administrative expenses against a higher sales base.&#160; A primary opportunity to increase our gross margin is to increase women&#146;s non-athletic sales as a percentage of our total business.&#160; Women&#146;s product has historically achieved the highest gross margin.&#160; To achieve this goal, we have improved the fashion content of our women&#146;s merchandise assortment, particularly in the dress and seasonal categories.&#160; Secondly, to highlight our women&#146;s merchandise, we introduced a new store design in 2003 that prominently displays women&#146;s footwear immediately upon entering the store.&#160; At the end of 2004, approximately 80 of our existing stores had the new design.&#160; Over the next four years, we intend to remodel virtually all remaining stores with
the latest store design.</font></p>
<p>
<font size="2" face="Times New Roman"><b>Merchandising</b></font></p>
<p>
<font size="2" face="Times New Roman">Our merchandising strategy is designed to provide a large selection of moderately priced footwear for the entire family.&#160; Our stores carry an average of approximately 29,600 pairs of shoes featuring a broad assortment of current-season name brand footwear, supplemented with private label merchandise and select name brand closeouts.&#160; Our stores also carry complementary accessories such as handbags, wallets, shoe care items and socks.&#160; The mix of merchandise and the brands offered in a particular store are based upon the demographics of each market, among other factors.</font></p>

<p><font size="2" face="Times New Roman">Our mic-person offers limited-duration promotions throughout the day, encouraging customers to take immediate advantage of value pricing.&#160; We emphasize name brand merchandise to customers with creative signage and by prominently displaying selected brands on end caps, focal walls and within the aisles.&#160; These displays may highlight a product offering of a single vendor or may make a seasonal or lifestyle statement by highlighting similar footwear from multiple vendors.&#160; These visual merchandise techniques make it easier for customers to shop and focus attention on key name brands.&#160; Expenses for signage and visual displays highlighting a particular brand typically will be partially or fully reimbursed by the vendor.</font></p>
<p>
<font size="2" face="Times New Roman">The table below sets forth our percentage of sales by product category for fiscal years 2004, 2003 and 2002.</font></p>

  <td  valign="top">
  <p>
<font size="2" face="Times New Roman">(1)&nbsp;&nbsp;Children&#146;s includes children&#146;s   athletic shoes.</font></p>  </td>
 </tr>
 <tr>
  <td  valign="top">
  <p>
<font size="2" face="Times New Roman">(2)&nbsp;&nbsp;Includes men&#146;s and women&#146;s sizes   only.</font></p>  </td>

</table>



<p><font size="2" face="Times New Roman">Women&#146;s, men&#146;s and children&#146;s non-athletic footwear categories are further divided into dress, casual, sport, sandals and boots.&#160; Athletic shoes are classified by functionality, such as running, basketball or fitness shoes.&#160; In 2004, athletic styles, including children&#146;s sizes, represented slightly more than half of our footwear sales.</font></p>
<p>
<font size="2" face="Times New Roman"><b>Pricing</b></font></p>
<p>
<font size="2" face="Times New Roman">Our pricing strategy is designed to emphasize value.&#160; By combining current season name brand product with promotional pricing, we feel that we create a better value for customers.&#160; Initial pricing decisions are guided by gross profit margin targets, which vary by merchandise category and depend on whether the item is name brand or private label merchandise.&#160; Markdowns are centrally managed by the buying staff and communicated to the stores through information systems as needed.</font></p>
<p>
<font size="2" face="Times New Roman">In-store signage is used extensively to highlight sales promotions and to advertise promotional pricing to meet or beat competitors&#146; sale prices.</font></p>
<p  align="center">
<font size="2" face="Times New Roman">3</font></p>



<div style="page-break-before:always"></div>
<PAGE>
<br>



<p>
<font size="2" face="Times New Roman"><b>Advertising and Promotion</b></font></p>
<p>
<font size="2" face="Times New Roman">In-store promotions are a key element in our marketing effort.&#160; By utilizing both planned and impromptu contests and games, store managers create an environment that encourages customer interaction with store personnel.&#160; For example, with a spin of the Spin-N-Win&#153; Wheel, a customer is enticed to purchase a second pair of shoes by winning an on-the-spot discount.&#160; Promotions of this type exemplify our emphasis on fun and excitement in order to enhance our customers&#146; total shopping experience.</font></p>

<p><font size="2" face="Times New Roman">We use various forms of media advertising to communicate the exceptional values offered on specific shoes or entire product categories.&#160; Approximately 55% of our total advertising budget was directed to television and radio in 2004.&#160; Print media (including newspaper ads, inserts and direct mail) and outdoor advertising account for the balance of the budget.&#160; A special effort is made to utilize the cooperative advertising dollars offered by vendors whenever possible.&#160; Major promotions during grand openings and peak selling periods allow customers to win prizes such as electronics, gift cards, merchandise or cash.</font></p>
<p>
<font size="2" face="Times New Roman">We strive to make each store opening a major retail event.&#160; Grand openings feature contests, cash and prize giveaways, and live musical performances.&#160; We believe our grand openings help to establish the high-energy, promotional atmosphere that develops a loyal, repeat customer base and generates word-of-mouth advertising.</font></p>
<p>
<font size="2" face="Times New Roman"><b>Store Location and Design</b></font></p>
<p>
<font size="2" face="Times New Roman">The number of stores opened and closed for fiscal years 2004, 2003 and 2002 were as follows:</font></p>

  <p>
<font size="1" face="Times New Roman">&nbsp;</font></p>  </td>

<p><font size="2" face="Times New Roman">At January 29, 2005, we had 255 stores located in 24 states, primarily in the Midwest, South and Southeast regions of the United States.&#160; Although three stores are located in enclosed malls, we prefer strip shopping center locations where occupancy costs are typically lower and we enjoy greater operating freedom to implement our non-traditional retail methods.&#160; We feel that our target customers enjoy the convenience offered by strip shopping centers as opposed to enclosed malls.</font></p>
<p>
<font size="2" face="Times New Roman">All of our stores are leased rather than owned.&#160; We believe the flexibility afforded by leasing allows us to avoid the inherent risks of owning real estate, particularly with respect to under-performing stores. &#160;Before entering a new market, we perform a market, demographic and competition analysis to evaluate the suitability of the potential market.&#160; Potential store site selection criteria include, among other factors, market demographics, traffic counts, the tenant mix of a potential strip shopping center, visibility within the center and from major thoroughfares, overall retail activity of the area and proposed lease terms.&#160; The time required to open a store after signing a lease depends primarily upon the landlord&#146;s ability to deliver the premises.&#160; After we accept the premises from the landlord, we can generally open a store within 45 days.</font></p>
<p>
<font size="2" face="Times New Roman">Critical to the success of opening new stores in larger markets or geographic areas is our ability to cluster stores.&#160; Clustering involves the operation of multiple locations in a particular metropolitan area or in several smaller markets located in reasonable proximity to one another.&#160; The clustering of stores creates cost efficiencies by enabling us to leverage store expenses with respect to advertising, distribution and management costs.</font></p>
<p>
<font size="2" face="Times New Roman">As of January 29, 2005, our stores averaged approximately 11,600 square feet, ranging in size from 6,500 to 26,500 square feet.&#160; Our current store prototype utilizes between 8,000 and 12,000 square feet, depending upon, among other factors, the location of the store and the population base the store is expected to service.&#160; The sales area of most stores is approximately 85% of the gross store size.</font></p>

<p><font size="2" face="Times New Roman">Our stores are designed and fixtured to reflect the high energy level of our retail concept.&#160; Stores are typically equipped with a sound system, microphone and entertainment devices such as the Spin-N-Win&#153; Wheel.&#160; With an open stock format, merchandise is displayed by category, with athletic footwear located in the center of the store to provide a transition between women&#146;s and men&#146;s footwear. </font></p>
<p  align="center">
<font size="2" face="Times New Roman">4</font></p>



<div style="page-break-before:always"></div>
<PAGE>
<br>



<p>
<font size="2" face="Times New Roman"><b><i>Updated Store Design</i></b>.&#160; The store design and logo utilized by 70% of our stores was introduced in 1996.&#160; This design conveys a carnival-like atmosphere through the use of distinctive signs, flashing colored lights, large mirrors and bold colors.&#160; While we believe the existing design will continue to be successful into the future, a new store design was developed and rolled out in all stores opened and remodeled in 2003 and after.&#160; The new design incorporates the excitement and energy that makes Shoe Carnival distinctive, but features a contemporary look and feel by utilizing a more muted color scheme, larger-than-life sized graphics, better visual displays and an improved wayfinding system.&#160; In addition, the Shoe Carnival logo was redesigned to reflect the store&#146;s new color scheme and contemporary look.&#160; </font></p>
<p>
<font size="2" face="Times New Roman">The new design highlights our women&#146;s non-athletic merchandise by relocating from an interior position to the front window line.&#160; We also showcase our latest women&#146;s styles on displays which can be seen by customers as they enter the store.&#160; At the end of 2004, approximately 80 of our stores had the new design.&#160; Over the next four years, we intend to remodel virtually all remaining stores to the latest store design.&#160; Prominently displaying the women&#146;s merchandise is one way in which we expect to achieve our goal of raising the percent of sales of women&#146;s merchandise from 23% in 2004 to between 28% and 30% over the next five years.</font></p>
<p>
<font size="2" face="Times New Roman"><b>Store Operations</b></font></p>
<p>
<font size="2" face="Times New Roman">Management of store operations is the responsibility of our Executive Vice President - Store Operations, who is assisted by divisional senior vice presidents, regional managers and the individual store general managers.&#160; There are two divisions designated as the North and South Divisions.&#160; Each divisional senior vice president is responsible for approximately twelve regions, but is ultimately expected to manage up to fifteen regions.&#160; Each regional manager is responsible for the operation of between six and seventeen stores and is required to visit each store periodically, concentrating more heavily on under-performing stores.&#160; Regional managers meet with their respective divisional senior vice president and other members of senior management on a periodic basis to discuss strategies, merchandise, advertising, financial performance and personnel requirements.</font></p>

<p><font size="2" face="Times New Roman">Each store has a general manager and up to three assistant managers, depending on sales volume.&#160; General managers and most assistant managers are paid a salary, while all other store employees are paid on an hourly basis.&#160; We provide an incentive compensation plan for regional and general managers based primarily upon the attainment of sales, expense control and profitability goals.</font></p>
<p>
<font size="2" face="Times New Roman">Administrative functions are centrally controlled from corporate headquarters.&#160; These functions include accounting, purchasing, store maintenance, information systems, advertising, human resources, distribution and pricing.&#160; Regional and general managers are expected and encouraged to provide feedback to all corporate departments to improve efficiencies.&#160; Regional and general managers are charged with making certain merchandising decisions necessary to maximize sales and profits primarily through merchandise placement, signage and timely clearance of slower selling items.</font></p>
<p>
<font size="2" face="Times New Roman"><b>Distribution</b></font></p>
<p>
<font size="2" face="Times New Roman">We operate a single 200,000 square foot distribution facility in Evansville, Indiana.&#160; The distribution center processes virtually all merchandise prior to shipping to the stores.&#160; At a minimum, this includes count verification, price and bar code labeling of each unit (when not performed by the manufacturer), redistribution of an order into size assortments and allocation of shipments to individual stores.&#160; Once a distribution order form is received from the buying staff, the remainder of the distribution process, including packing, allocating, storing and shipping is essentially paperless.&#160; Merchandise is shipped to each store from one to two times per week, depending on store volume, proximity to other stores and proximity to the distribution center.&#160; The majority of shipments are handled by a dedicated carrier, with occasional use of common carriers.</font></p>
<p>
<font size="2" face="Times New Roman">In 2004, we completed a forward-looking logistics study evaluating the need for additional distribution center capacity as we grow.&#160; Based on our current store growth plans, the results of the study identified the need to have additional distribution capacity available by the end of 2006.&#160; We intend to replace our existing 200,000 square foot distribution center with a new 400,000 square foot facility.&#160; The construction is anticipated to begin in the spring of 2006.&#160; Preliminary cost estimates for land, building and equipment are expected to range from $23 million to $25 million.</font></p>

<p  align="center"><font size="2" face="Times New Roman">5</font></p>



<div style="page-break-before:always"></div>
<PAGE>
<br>



<p>
<font size="2" face="Times New Roman"><b>Buying Operations</b></font></p>
<p>
<font size="2" face="Times New Roman">Maintaining fresh, fashionable merchandise is critical to our success.&#160; Our buyers stay in touch with evolving trends by shopping fashion-leading markets, attending national trade shows, gathering vendor input and monitoring the current styles shown in leading fashion and lifestyle magazines.&#160; Management of the purchasing function is the responsibility of our Executive Vice President - General Merchandise Manager.&#160; Regional managers are expected to provide input to our merchandising staff regarding market specific fashion trends.</font></p>
<p>
<font size="2" face="Times New Roman">We purchase merchandise from over 160 footwear vendors.&#160; In 2004, three suppliers, Nike USA, Inc., Reebok International Ltd., and Skechers USA, Inc. each accounted for more than 10% of net sales and together accounted for approximately 36% of net sales.&#160; A loss of any of our key suppliers in certain product categories could have a material adverse effect on our business.&#160; As is common in the industry, we do not have any long-term contracts with suppliers.</font></p>
<p>
<font size="2" face="Times New Roman"><b>Information Systems</b></font></p>
<p>
<font size="2" face="Times New Roman">We have devoted significant resources to expand our sophisticated information technology systems.&#160; Our network connects our corporate office to every store, providing up-to-date sales and inventory information as required.&#160; Each store has an independent point-of-sale controller, with two to 12 point-of-sale terminals per store.&#160; To provide maximum flexibility and maintain data integrity, our information systems are based upon relational database technology.&#160; Our distribution facility utilizes a spread spectrum radio frequency network to assure accurate, real-time information throughout the distribution operation.&#160; Each member of the buying and distribution staff has on-line access to up-to-date sales and inventory information broken down by store, style, color, size and width.&#160; Additional data analysis can be quickly provided on demand by using either a fourth generation language programming tool or personal computer tools that access

our database.</font></p>

<p><font size="2" face="Times New Roman">A state of the art point-of-sales system uses bar code technology to capture sales, gross margin and inventory information.&#160; The system provides, in addition to other features, full price management (including price look-up), promotion tracking capabilities (in support of the spontaneous nature of the in-store price promotions), real-time sales and gross margin analysis by product category at the store level and customer tracking.</font></p>
<p>
<font size="2" face="Times New Roman"><b>Competition</b></font></p>
<p>
<font size="2" face="Times New Roman">The retail footwear business is highly competitive.&#160; We believe the principal competitive factors in our industry are merchandise selection, price, fashion, quality, location, store environment and service.&#160; We compete primarily with department stores, shoe stores, sporting goods stores and mass merchandisers.</font></p>
<p>
<font size="2" face="Times New Roman">We compete with most department stores and traditional shoe stores by offering lower prices.&#160; We compete with off-price retailers, mass merchandisers and discount stores by offering a wider and deeper selection of merchandise.</font></p>
<p>
<font size="2" face="Times New Roman">Many of our competitors are significantly larger and have substantially greater financial and other resources.&#160; However, we believe that our distinctive retail format, in combination with our wide merchandise selection, competitive prices and low operating costs, have in the past enabled us to compete effectively.</font></p>
<p>
<font size="2" face="Times New Roman"><b>Employees</b></font></p>
<p>
<font size="2" face="Times New Roman">At January 29, 2005, we had approximately 3,770 employees, of which approximately 2,130 were employed on a part-time or seasonal basis.&#160; The number of employees fluctuates during the year primarily due to seasonality.&#160; None of our employees are represented by a labor union.</font></p>
<p>
<font size="2" face="Times New Roman">We attribute a large portion of our success in various areas of cost control to our inclusion of virtually all management level employees in incentive compensation plans.&#160; We contribute all or a portion of the cost of medical, disability and life insurance coverage for those employees who are eligible to participate in company-sponsored plans.&#160; Additionally, we sponsor a 401(k) retirement plan which is open to all employees who have met the minimum age and workhour requirements.&#160; All employees are eligible to receive discounts on purchases from our stores.&#160; We consider our relationship with our employees to be satisfactory.</font></p>

<p  align="center"><font size="2" face="Times New Roman">6</font></p>



<div style="page-break-before:always"></div>
<PAGE>
<br>



<p>
<font size="2" face="Times New Roman"><b>Trademarks</b></font></p>
<p>
<font size="2" face="Times New Roman">We own the following federally registered trademarks and servicemarks:&nbsp;&nbsp;Shoe Carnival<sup>&#174;</sup>, The Carnival<sup>&#174;</sup>, Nuff Said<sup>&#174;</sup>, Donna Lawrence<sup>&#174;</sup>, Oak Meadow<sup>&#174;</sup>, Victoria Spenser<sup>&#174;</sup>, Via Nova<sup>&#174;</sup>, Fresh Stuff<sup>&#174;</sup>, Innocence<sup>&#174;</sup>, Trade Dress<sup>&#174;</sup>, Carnival Lites<sup>&#174; </sup>and Color Block Design<sup>&#174;</sup>. We believe these marks are valuable and, accordingly, intend to maintain the marks and the related registrations.&#160; We are not aware of any pending claims of infringement or other challenges to our right to use these marks.</font></p>
<p>
<font size="2" face="Times New Roman"><b>Risk Factors</b></font></p>
<p>
<font size="2" face="Times New Roman">You should carefully consider the following risk factors and all other information contained in this annual report before making an investment decision with respect to our common stock.&#160; Investing in our common stock involves a high degree of risk.&#160; If any of the following risks actually occurs, we may not be able to conduct our business as currently planned and our financial condition and operating results could be seriously harmed.&#160; See &#147;BUSINESS - Forward-Looking Statements.&#148;</font></p>
<p>
<font size="2" face="Times New Roman"><b><i>We may not be able to successfully execute our growth strategy, which could have a material adverse effect on our business, financial condition and results of operations.</i></b>&#160; We intend to open new stores as a part of our growth strategy.&#160; We may not be able to open all of the new stores contemplated by our growth strategy and the new stores that we open may not be as profitable as existing stores.</font></p>
<p>
<font size="2" face="Times New Roman">The complexity of our operations and management responsibilities will increase as we grow.&#160; Our growth strategy requires that we continue to expand and improve our operating and financial systems and to expand, train and manage our employee base.&#160; In addition, as we open new stores, we may be unable to hire a sufficient number of qualified store personnel or successfully integrate the new stores into our business.</font></p>

<p><font size="2" face="Times New Roman">The success of our growth strategy will depend on a number of other factors, many of which are out of our control, including, among other things:</font></p>

<p align="center"><font size="2" face="Times New Roman">7</font></p>



<div style="page-break-before:always"></div>
<PAGE>
<br>



<p>
<font size="2" face="Times New Roman">Declines or fluctuations in our comparable store sales could cause the trading price of our common stock to decline.</font></p>
<p>
<font size="2" face="Times New Roman"><b><i>We depend on our key suppliers for merchandise and advertising support and the loss of key suppliers could adversely affect our business.</i></b>&#160; Our business depends upon our ability to purchase fashionable, name brand and other merchandise at competitive prices from our suppliers.&#160; In 2004, three branded suppliers, Nike USA, Inc., Reebok International Ltd. and Skechers USA, Inc., each accounted for more than 10% of our net sales and together accounted for approximately 36% of net sales.&#160; Name brand suppliers also provide us with cooperative advertising and visual merchandising funds.&#160; A loss of any of our key suppliers in certain product categories could have a material adverse effect on our business.&#160; Although our relations with key suppliers are currently satisfactory and we have adequate sources of name brand and other merchandise at competitive prices, we cannot assure you that we will be able to acquire such merchandise at
comparable prices or on comparable terms in the future.&#160; As is common in the industry, we do not have any long-term contracts with our suppliers.</font></p>

<p><font size="2" face="Times New Roman"><b><i>An increase in the cost or a disruption in the flow of our imported goods may decrease our sales and profits.</i></b>&#160; We rely on imported goods to sell in our stores.&#160; Substantially all of the footwear product we sell is manufactured overseas, including the merchandise we import directly from overseas manufacturers and agents and the merchandise we purchase from domestic vendors.&#160; The primary footwear manufacturers are located in China, Brazil and East Asia.&#160; A disruption in the flow of imported merchandise or an increase in the cost of those goods may decrease our sales and profits.</font></p>
<p>
<font size="2" face="Times New Roman">If imported merchandise becomes more expensive or unavailable, the transition to alternative sources may not occur in time to meet our demands.&#160; Products from alternative sources may be of lesser quality and more expensive than those we currently import.&#160; Other risks associated with our use of imported goods include:&#160; disruptions in the flow of imported goods because of factors such as electricity or raw material shortages, work stoppages, strikes and political unrest; problems with oceanic shipping, including shipping container shortages; economic crises and international disputes; increases in the cost of purchasing or shipping foreign merchandise resulting from the failure to maintain normal trade relations with source countries; import duties, import quotas and other trade sanctions; and increases in shipping rates imposed by the trans-Pacific shipping cartel.</font></p>
<p>
<font size="2" face="Times New Roman"><b><i>Our failure to identify fashion trends could result in lower sales, higher markdowns and lower gross profits.</i></b>&#160; Our success depends upon our ability to anticipate and react to the fashion tastes of our customers and provide merchandise that satisfies customer demand.&#160; Our failure to anticipate, identify or react appropriately to changes in consumer fashion preferences may result in lower sales, higher markdowns to reduce excess inventories and lower gross profits.&#160; Conversely, if we fail to anticipate or react to consumer demand for our products, we may experience inventory shortages, which would result in lost sales and could negatively impact our customer goodwill, our brand image and our profitability.&#160; Moreover, our business relies on continuous changes in fashion preferences.&#160; Stagnating consumer preferences could also result in lower sales and would require us to take higher markdowns to reduce excess
inventories.</font></p>

<p><font size="2" face="Times New Roman"><b><i>Our failure to retain our existing senior management team and to continue to attract qualified personnel could adversely affect our business.</i></b>&#160; Our success depends to a large extent on the continued service of our executive management team.&#160; Departures by executive officers could have a negative impact on our business, as we may not be able to find suitable management personnel to replace departing executives on a timely basis.&#160; Furthermore, our strategy requires us to continue to train, motivate and manage our employees and to attract, motivate and retain additional qualified managerial and merchandising personnel.&#160; Competition for these types of personnel is intense, and we cannot assure you that we will be successful in attracting, assimilating and retaining the personnel required to grow and operate our business profitably.</font></p>
<p>
<font size="2" face="Times New Roman"><b><i>We face significant competition in our markets and we may be unable to compete favorably.</i></b>&#160; The retail footwear industry is highly competitive.&#160; We compete primarily with department stores, shoe stores, sporting goods stores and mass merchandisers.&#160; Many of our competitors have greater financial resources than we do.&#160; Economic pressures on or bankruptcies of vendors could result in increased pricing pressures.&#160; This competition could adversely affect our results of operations and financial condition in the future.&#160; Our stores compete, among other things, on the basis of convenience of location and store layout, product mix and selection, customer convenience and price.</font></p>
<p>
<font size="2" face="Times New Roman"><b><i>We will require significant funds to implement our growth strategy and meet our other liquidity needs.</i></b>&#160; We cannot assure you that we will be able to generate sufficient cash flow from operations or obtain sufficient borrowings under our existing credit agreement to finance our growth strategy and meet our other liquidity needs.&#160; In 2005, capital expenditures, including assets acquired through leasing arrangements but net of lease incentives, are expected to range from $12&nbsp;million to $14&nbsp;million.&#160; Our actual costs may be greater than anticipated.&#160; We also require working capital to support inventory for our existing stores.&#160; Failure to generate or raise sufficient funds may require us to modify, delay or abandon some of our future growth or expenditure plans.&#160; Also, our results would be adversely affected if interest rates materially increase from present levels.</font></p>

<p  align="center"><font size="2" face="Times New Roman">8</font></p>



<div style="page-break-before:always"></div>
<PAGE>
<br>



<p>
<font size="2" face="Times New Roman"><b><i>Declines in general economic conditions and fluctuations in consumer confidence and spending could lead to reduced consumer demand for our products.</i></b>&#160; General economic conditions and consumer confidence and spending can decline as a result of numerous factors outside of our control such as rising oil prices, terrorist attacks, acts of war and natural disasters.&#160; While the precise effects of these events on our industry and business are difficult to determine, they could lead to reduced consumer demand for our products and it is possible that they may have an adverse effect on our operating and financial performance.</font></p>
<p>
<font size="2" face="Times New Roman"><b><i>We would be adversely affected if our distribution or IT operations were disrupted.</i></b>&#160; We operate a single, 200,000 square foot distribution center in Evansville, Indiana.&#160; Virtually all merchandise received by our stores, with the exception of a small amount of goods shipped directly to the stores, is shipped through our distribution center.&#160; Our corporate computer network is essential to our distribution process.&#160; If our distribution center is shut down for any reason, such as a national disaster, power outage or terrorist attack, or if our information technology systems do not operate effectively, we could incur significantly higher costs and longer lead times associated with distributing our products to our stores.&#160; We maintain insurance to protect us from the costs relating to matters such as a shutdown, but we cannot assure you that our insurance will be sufficient, or that the insurance proceeds will be timely paid to
us, in the event of a shutdown.</font></p>
<p>
<font size="2" face="Times New Roman"><b><i>Failure to maintain effective internal control over financial reporting could result in a loss of investor confidence in our financial reports and have a material adverse effect on our stock price.</i></b>&#160; We must continue to document, test and evaluate our internal control over financial reporting in order to satisfy the requirements of Section&nbsp;404 of the Sarbanes-Oxley Act of 2002, which requires annual reports by management regarding the effectiveness of our internal control over financial reporting and a report by our independent registered public accounting firm attesting to management&#146;s assessment and the effectiveness of the internal control.&#160; We&#160; have, and expect that we will continue to, expend significant management time and resources documenting and testing our internal control over financial reporting.&#160; While management&#146;s evaluation as of January 29, 2005 resulted in the conclusion that our internal control
over financial reporting was effective as of that date, we cannot predict the outcome of testing in future periods.&#160; If we conclude in future periods that our internal control over financial reporting is not effective, it could result in lost investor confidence in the accuracy, reliability and completeness of our financial reports.&#160; Any such events could have a material adverse effect on our stock price. </font></p>

<p><font size="2" face="Times New Roman"><b><i>We are controlled by our principal shareholder.</i></b>&#160; J. Wayne Weaver, our Chairman of the Board of Directors and principal shareholder, his spouse and his adult children together own approximately 47% of our outstanding common stock.&#160; Accordingly, Mr.&nbsp;Weaver is able to exert substantial influence over our management and operations.&#160; In addition, his interests may differ from or be opposed to the interests of our other shareholders, and his control may have the effect of delaying or preventing a change in control that may be favored by other shareholders.</font></p>
<p>
<font size="2" face="Times New Roman"><b><i>Our stock price may be volatile and could decline substantially.</i></b>&#160; The stock market has, from time to time, experienced extreme price and volume fluctuations.&#160; Many factors may cause the market price for our common stock to decline, including: </font></p>

<p><font size="2" face="Times New Roman">In the past, companies that have experienced volatility in the market price of their stock have been the subject of securities class action litigation.&#160; If we become involved in a securities class action litigation in the future, it could result in substantial costs and diversion of management attention and resources, thus harming our business.</font></p>

<p align="center"><font size="2" face="Times New Roman">9</font></p>



<div style="page-break-before:always"></div>
<PAGE>
<br>

<p><font size="2" face="Times New Roman"><b><i>Our quarterly  operating  results will  fluctuate due to  seasonality  and other
factors.</i></b>&#160;  Our quarterly  results of operations  have fluctuated in
the past and can be expected to continue to fluctuate in the  future.&#160;  Our
quarterly   results  of  operations  are  affected  by  a  variety  of  factors,
including:</font></p>

<p><font size="2" face="Times New Roman">We have three distinct peak selling periods: Easter, back-to-school and Christmas.&#160; To prepare for our peak shopping seasons, we must order and keep in stock significantly more merchandise than we would carry during other parts of the year.&#160; Any unanticipated decrease in demand for our products during these peak shopping seasons could require us to sell excess inventory at a substantial markdown, which could reduce our net sales and gross margins and negatively impact our profitability.&#160; Our operating results depend significantly upon the sales generated during these periods.&#160; If our future quarterly results fail to meet the expectations of research analysts, then the market price of our common stock could decline substantially.</font></p>

<p><font size="2" face="Times New Roman"><b><i>Provisions of our organizational documents and Indiana law might deter acquisition bids for us.</i></b>&#160; Our Restated Articles of Incorporation and Indiana corporate laws contain provisions that may discourage other persons from attempting to acquire control of us, including, without limitation, a Board of Directors that has staggered terms for its members, supermajority voting provisions, restrictions on the ability of shareholders to call a special meeting of shareholders and procedural requirements in connection with shareholder proposals or director nominations.&#160; The Board of Directors has the authority to issue preferred stock in one or more series without the approval of the holders of the common stock.&#160; In certain circumstances, the fact that corporate devices are in place which inhibit or discourage takeover attempts could reduce the market value of the common stock.</font></p>
<table align="center"  border="0" style="border-collapse:collapse" cellspacing="0"  cellpadding="0"  width="100%">

 <tr>
  <td width="10%" valign="top">
  <p>
<a name="sc_03"></a><font size="2" face="Times New Roman">ITEM 2.</font></p>  </td>
  <td width="90%" valign="top">
  <p>
<font size="2" face="Times New Roman"><b>PROPERTIES</b></font></p>  </td>
 </tr>
</table>



<p><font size="2" face="Times New Roman">We lease all existing stores and intend to lease all future stores.&#160; All leases for existing stores provide for fixed minimum rentals and most provide for contingent rental payments based upon various specified percentages of sales above minimum levels.&#160; Certain leases also contain escalation clauses for increases in minimum rentals, operating costs and taxes.</font></p>

<p><font size="2" face="Times New Roman"><i>Mr. Weaver</i> is Shoe Carnival&#146;s largest shareholder and has served as Chairman of the Board since March 1988.&#160; From 1978 until February 2, 1993, Mr. Weaver had served as president and chief executive officer of Nine West Group Inc., a designer, developer and marketer of women&#146;s footwear.&#160; He has over 40 years of experience in the footwear industry.&#160; Mr. Weaver is a former director of Nine West Group, Inc.&#160; Mr. Weaver serves as chairman and chief executive officer of Jacksonville Jaguars, LTD and chairman and chief executive officer of LC Footwear, LLC.</font></p>
<p>
<font size="2" face="Times New Roman"><i>Mr. Lemond</i> has been employed as President and Chief Executive Officer since September 1996.&#160; From March 1988 to September 1996, Mr. Lemond served as Executive Vice President, Chief Financial Officer, Treasurer and Assistant Secretary.&#160; On February 3, 1994, Mr. Lemond was promoted to the position of Chief Operating Officer.&#160; Mr. Lemond has served as a director since March 1988.&#160; Prior to March 1988, he served in similar officer capacities with Russell&#146;s&#160; Shoe Biz, Inc.&#160; Prior to joining Russell&#146;s Shoe Biz, Inc. in 1987, Mr. Lemond was a partner with a public accounting firm.&#160; He is a Certified Public Accountant.</font></p>

<p><font size="2" face="Times New Roman"><i>Mr. Baker</i> has been employed as Executive Vice President - Store Operations since June 2001.&#160; From March 1994 to June 2001, Mr. Baker served as Senior Vice President - Store Operations.&#160; From May 1992 to March 1994, Mr. Baker served as Vice President - Store Operations.&#160; Prior to that time, he served as one of our regional managers.&#160; From 1983 to June 1989, Mr. Baker held various retail management positions with Payless ShoeSource.</font></p>
<p>
<font size="2" face="Times New Roman"><i>Mr. Jackson</i> has been employed as Executive Vice President - Chief Financial Officer and Treasurer since August 2004. From June 2001 to August 2004, Mr. Jackson served as Senior Vice President &#150; Chief Financial Officer and Treasurer. From September 1996 to June 2001, Mr. Jackson served as Vice President - Chief Financial Officer and Treasurer.&#160; From January 1993 to September 1996, Mr. Jackson served as Vice President - Controller and Chief Accounting Officer.&#160; Prior to January 1993, Mr. Jackson held various accounting positions with us.&#160; Prior to joining us in 1988, Mr. Jackson was associated with a public accounting firm.&#160; He is a Certified Public Accountant. </font></p>
<p>
<font size="2" face="Times New Roman"><i>Mr. Sifford</i> has been employed as Executive Vice President - General Merchandise Manager since June 2001.&#160; From April 13, 1997 to June 2001, Mr. Sifford served as Senior Vice President - General Merchandise Manager.&#160; Prior to joining us, Mr. Sifford served as merchandise manager-shoes for Belk Store Services, Inc.</font></p>
<p>
<font size="2" face="Times New Roman"><i>Mr. Kapp</i> has been employed with us since March 1988, most recently as our Vice President &#150; Planning / Analysis and Secretary.&#160; Prior to assuming his current position, Mr. Kapp held various accounting and retail positions with us.</font></p>
<p>
<font size="2" face="Times New Roman">Our executive officers serve at the discretion of the Board of Directors.&#160; There is no family relationship between any of the directors or executive officers.</font></p>

<p>
<font size="2" face="Times New Roman">We do not currently intend to pay cash dividends on our Common Stock in the foreseeable future.&#160; The payment of any future dividends will be at the discretion of our Board of Directors and will depend upon, among other things, future earnings, operations, capital requirements, our general financial condition and general business conditions.&#160; In addition, our credit agreement contains certain limitations on the payment of dividends.</font></p>

<p><font size="2" face="Times New Roman">No unregistered equity securities were sold by us during fiscal 2004.</font></p>

<table align="center"  border="0" style="border-collapse:collapse" cellspacing="0"  cellpadding="0"  width="100%">

 <tr>
  <td width="10%" valign="top">
  <p>
<a name="sc_09"></a><font size="2" face="Times New Roman">ITEM 7.</font></p>  </td>
  <td width="90%" valign="top">
  <p>
<font size="2" face="Times New Roman"><b>MANAGEMENT&#146;S DISCUSSION AND   ANALYSIS OF FINANCIAL CONDITION AND RESULTS OF OPERATIONS</b></font></p>  </td>
 </tr>
</table>



<p><font size="2" face="Times New Roman">The following discussion of our financial condition and results of operations should be read together with our consolidated financial statements and notes to those statements included in PART II, ITEM 8 of the Form 10-K.</font></p>
<p>
<font size="2" face="Times New Roman"><b>Overview</b></font></p>
<p>
<font size="2" face="Times New Roman">Shoe Carnival, Inc. is one of the nation&#146;s largest family footwear retailers.&#160; As of January 29, 2005, we operated 255 stores in 24 states in the Midwest, South and Southeast regions of the United States.&#160; We offer a distinctive shopping experience, a broad merchandise assortment and value to our customers while maintaining an efficient store level cost structure.&#160; </font></p>
<p>
<font size="2" face="Times New Roman">Our stores combine competitive pricing with a highly promotional, in-store marketing effort that encourages customer participation and creates a fun and exciting shopping experience.&#160; We believe this highly promotional atmosphere results in various competitive advantages, including increased multiple unit sales; the building of a loyal, repeat customer base; the creation of word-of-mouth advertising; and enhanced sell through of in-season goods.&#160; Our objective is to be the destination store-of-choice for a wide range of consumers seeking moderately priced, current season name brand and private label footwear.&#160; Our product assortment includes dress and casual shoes, sandals, boots and a wide assortment of athletic shoes for the entire family.&#160; We believe that by offering a wide selection of both athletic and non-athletic footwear, we are able to reduce our exposure to shifts in fashion preferences between those categories.&#160; Our ability to
identify and react to fashion changes is a key factor in our sales and earnings performance.</font></p>

<p><font size="2" face="Times New Roman">Our marketing effort targets middle income, value-conscious consumers seeking name brand footwear for all age groups.&#160; We believe that by offering a wide selection of popular styles of name brand merchandise at competitive prices, we generate broad customer appeal.&#160; Our cost-efficient store operations and real estate strategy enable us to price products competitively and earn attractive store level returns.&#160; Low labor costs are achieved by housing merchandise directly on the selling floor in an open-stock format, enabling customers who choose to serve themselves.&#160; This reduces the staffing required to assist customers and reduces store level labor costs as a percentage of sales.&#160; We prefer to locate stores predominantly in strip shopping centers in order to take advantage of lower occupancy costs and maximize our exposure to value-oriented shoppers.</font></p>
<p>
<font size="2" face="Times New Roman">Our fiscal year consists of a 52/53 week period ending on the Saturday closest to January 31.&#160; Unless otherwise stated, references to the years 2004, 2003 and 2002 relate respectively to the fiscal years ended January 29, 2005, January 31, 2004, and February 1, 2003.&#160; Fiscal years 2004, 2003 and 2002 consisted of 52 weeks.</font></p>
<p>
<font size="2" face="Times New Roman"><b>Restatement of Financial Statements</b></font></p>
<p>
<font size="2" face="Times New Roman">On February 7, 2005, the Office of the Chief Accountant of the U.S. Securities and Exchange Commission (the &#147;SEC&#148;) released a letter expressing the SEC&#146;s views on certain lease accounting matters as they relate to the application of generally accepted accounting principles (&#147;GAAP&#148;).&#160; Based on these views we, along with numerous other retailers, reviewed our lease accounting practices and determined that our accounting for the lease commencement date was not consistent with GAAP.&#160; </font></p>
<p>
<font size="2" face="Times New Roman">
Historically, and consistent with practice prevalent throughout the retailing
industry, we recognized straight line rent expense for leases beginning on the
earlier of rent commencement date or the store opening date.&#160; This had the
effect of excluding the build-out period of our stores from the calculation of
the period over which we expense rent.&#160; We have now changed this practice
to include the build-out period in our calculation of rent expense.&#160; While
this change results in an acceleration of the commencement of rent expense for
each lease,  the total rent due under the lease remains unchanged and
is amortized over a greater number of months.&#160; This change has no effect on
historical or future cash flows or the timing or amounts of payments under
related leases.</font></p>

<p><font size="2" face="Times New Roman">We believe it is appropriate to align our historical financial statements with the SEC&#146;s comments on lease accounting under GAAP, and we have done so, as reflected in our decision to restate results for prior periods.&#160; This restatement reduced net income by $256,000 and $174,000 for fiscal years 2003 and 2002, respectively, and resulted in a $1.1 million reduction in retained earnings at the beginning of fiscal year 2002.</font></p>
<p>
<font size="2" face="Times New Roman">Note 3 to the Consolidated  Financial Statements contains a&#160; summary of the
effects of the  restatement on our  Consolidated  Balance  Sheet,  Consolidated
Statements of Income and  Consolidated  Statements of Cash Flows for the periods
presented.</font></p>
<p  align="center">
<font size="2" face="Times New Roman">15</font></p>



<div style="page-break-before:always"></div>
<PAGE>
<br>

<p>
<font size="2" face="Times New Roman">The accompanying  Management  Discussion and Analysis includes the impact of these restatements.</font></p>

<p>
<font size="2" face="Times New Roman"><b>Critical Accounting Policies</b></font></p>
<p>
<font size="2" face="Times New Roman">It is necessary for us to include certain judgements in our reported financial results.&#160; These judgements involve estimates that are inherently uncertain and actual results could differ materially from these estimates.&#160; The accounting policies that require the more significant judgements are:</font></p>
<p>
<font size="2" face="Times New Roman"><i>Merchandise Inventories</i> - Merchandise inventories are stated at the lower of cost or market using the first-in, first-out (FIFO) method.&#160; In determining market value, we estimate the future sales price of items of merchandise contained in the inventory as of the balance sheet date. Factors considered in this determination include, among others, current and recently recorded sales prices, the length of time product has been held in inventory and quantities of various product styles contained in inventory.&#160; The ultimate amount realized from the sale of certain product could differ materially from our estimates.&#160; We also estimate a shrinkage reserve for the period between the last physical count and the balance sheet date.&#160; The estimate for the shrinkage reserve can be affected by changes in merchandise mix and changes in actual shrinkage trends.</font></p>
<p>
<font size="2" face="Times New Roman"><i>Valuation of Long-Lived Assets</i> - We review long-lived assets whenever events or circumstances indicate the carrying value of an asset may not be recoverable and annually when no such event has occurred.&#160; We evaluate the ongoing value of assets associated with retail stores that have been open longer than one year. When undiscounted cash flows estimated to be generated by those assets are less than the carrying value of those assets, impairment losses are recorded. When events such as these occur, the impaired assets are adjusted to estimated fair value and an impairment loss is recorded in selling, general and administrative expenses.&#160; Our assumptions and estimates used in the evaluation of impairment, including current and future economic trends for stores, are subject to a high degree of judgement and if actual results or market conditions differ from those anticipated, additional losses may be recorded.</font></p>

<p><font size="2" face="Times New Roman"><i>Deferred Income Taxes</i> - We calculate income taxes in accordance with Statement of Financial Accounting Standards No. 109, &#147;Accounting for Income Taxes&#148;, which requires the use of the asset and liability method.&#160; Under this method, deferred tax assets and liabilities are recognized based on the difference between the consolidated financial statement carrying amounts of existing assets and liabilities and their respective tax basis.&#160; Deferred tax assets and liabilities are measured using the tax rates in effect in the years when those temporary differences are expected to reverse.&#160; Inherent in the measurement of these deferred balances are certain judgments and interpretations of existing tax law and other published guidance as applied to our operations.&#160; A valuation allowance has been provided for certain state operating losses recorded as a deferred tax asset.&#160; We anticipate that future taxable income, and prior year
taxable income during loss carryback periods, will not be sufficient to recover the full amount of deferred tax assets.&#160; Our effective tax rate considers our judgment of expected tax liabilities in the various taxing jurisdictions within which we are subject to tax.&#160; We have also been involved in tax audits.&#160; At any given time, multiple tax years are subject to audit by various taxing authorities.</font></p>
<p>
<font size="2" face="Times New Roman"><b>2005 Outlook</b></font></p>
<p>
<font size="2" face="Times New Roman">In 2004, we initiated certain changes to our business that we believe positively impacted our sales and earnings in the last half of 2004.&#160; We expect the changes will also benefit our 2005 financial performance.&#160; Certain of these changes are as follows:</font></p>
<p>
<font size="2" face="Times New Roman"><i>Store Openings</i> - Currently, we expect to open between 12 and 14 new stores and close four stores in 2005.&#160; These new stores will be located in large and small markets within our existing geographic areas.&#160; Our intention is to fill in certain under-penetrated larger markets with additional stores, thereby increasing the performance of the overall market.&#160; We also intend to enter smaller markets that we can fully penetrate with one or two stores.&#160; We generally can advertise more effectively in these markets, which helps to create immediate brand awareness.&#160; </font></p>

<p><font size="2" face="Times New Roman"><i>New Store Design</i> - In late 2002, we developed a new design for Shoe Carnival stores.&#160; This design, in addition to updating the color scheme, changed the placement of product on the sales floor, allowing us to place more emphasis on our women&#146;s non-athletic product.&#160; Prominently displaying the women&#146;s merchandise is one way in which we expect to achieve our goal of raising the percent of sales of women&#146;s merchandise from 23% of total sales in 2004 to between 28% an 30% over the next five years.</font></p>
<p  align="center">
<font size="2" face="Times New Roman">16</font></p>



<div style="page-break-before:always"></div>
<PAGE>
<br>



<p>
<font size="2" face="Times New Roman">Currently, we have about 80 stores with the new design.&#160; Over the next four years, we intend to remodel virtually all remaining stores to the latest store design.&#160; For 2005, we intend to fully remodel 10 stores and partially remodel another 30 stores.&#160; Additionally, we are going to roll out a new graphics package to all stores this year that will tie-in to our new advertising campaign.&#160; For remodels and the enhanced graphics package, we intend to spend approximately $5 million in 2005. </font></p>
<p>
<font size="2" face="Times New Roman">
<i>Merchandise</i> - Early in the spring of 2004, we recognized that our
non-athletic product mix was not as fashion-forward as it needed to be.&#160;
This was particularly true in our women&#146;s category.&#160; As a result,
beginning in the fall of 2004 and continuing into the spring of 2005, we
significantly increased the assortment of fashion footwear in both our
men&#146;s and women&#146;s non-athletic categories.</font></p>
<p>
<font size="2" face="Times New Roman"><i>Marketing</i> - In late 2004, we initiated a number of changes in our marketing program, including the hiring of a new advertising agency.&#160; A new television and radio advertising campaign was introduced during the 2005 Easter selling season with a fresh creative message that highlights the fashion and value of our improved women&#146;s product assortment.</font></p>
<p>
<font size="2" face="Times New Roman">We also changed our Grand Opening promotion for the 2005 new store openings, which highlights the women&#146;s side of our business and targets a woman consumer better than we did in prior years. </font></p>

<p><font size="2" face="Times New Roman"><b>Results of Operations</b></font></p>
<p>
<font size="2" face="Times New Roman">The following table sets forth our results of operations expressed as a percentage of net sales for the following fiscal years:</font></p>



<table align="center"  border="0" style="border-collapse:collapse" cellspacing="0"  cellpadding="0"  width="80%">

<p><font size="2" face="Times New Roman"><b>2004 Compared to 2003</b></font></p>
<p>
<font size="2" face="Times New Roman">Net Sales</font></p>
<p>
<font size="2" face="Times New Roman">Net sales increased $32.3 million to $590.2 million in 2004, a 5.8% increase over net sales of $557.9 million in 2003.&#160; The increase was attributable to the sales generated by the 22 stores opened in 2004 and the effect of a full year&#146;s worth of sales for the 37 stores opened in 2003, partially offset by the closing of four stores and a comparable store sales decrease of 0.8%.</font></p>
<p>
<font size="2" face="Times New Roman">
While sales of athletic footwear and accessories in our comparable stores
increased slightly for the year, our men&#146;s, women&#146;s and
children&#146;s non-athletic footwear categories each experienced declines in
comparable store sales.&#160; In the first two quarters of 2004 we saw declines
in our comparable store sales, but ended the year with back-to-back quarterly
comparable store sales gains. We believe the improved sales performance in the
second half of the year was at least partly due to enhancing the fashion content
of our merchandise offering and to improving the way we advertise to our
customers.</font></p>
<p  align="center">
<font size="2" face="Times New Roman">17</font></p>



<div style="page-break-before:always"></div>
<PAGE>
<br>



<p>
<font size="2" face="Times New Roman">Gross Profit</font></p>
<p>
<font size="2" face="Times New Roman">Gross profit increased $9.0 million to $167.2 million in 2004, a 5.7% increase from gross profit of $158.2 million in 2003.&#160; Our gross profit margin as a percentage of sales was 28.3% for fiscal 2004 as compared to 28.4% for fiscal 2003.&#160; As a percentage of sales, the merchandise margin increased 0.4% and buying, distribution and occupancy costs increased 0.5%.&#160; The increase in the merchandise margin as a percentage of sales was primarily driven by a decline in the amount of markdowns necessary to liquidate seasonal product.&#160; The increase in buying, distribution and occupancy costs as a percentage of sales was due to a lack of sales leverage on occupancy costs.</font></p>
<p>
<font size="2" face="Times New Roman">Selling, General and Administrative Expenses</font></p>
<p>
<font size="2" face="Times New Roman">Selling, general and administrative expenses increased $8.2 million to $146.4 million in 2004 from $138.2 million in 2003.&#160; As a percentage of sales, these expenses remained unchanged from the prior year at 24.8%.&#160; During fiscal 2004, we incurred $564,000 in asset retirement costs on store closings, relocations and remodels as compared to $789,000 in fiscal 2003.&#160; The aggregate of pre-opening expenses for the 22 new stores in 2004 was approximately $1.7 million, or 0.3% of sales, and was $2.5 million, or 0.4% of sales, for the 37 new stores in 2003.&#160; </font></p>

<p><font size="2" face="Times New Roman">Interest Expense</font></p>
<p>
<font size="2" face="Times New Roman">Interest expense decreased to $658,000 (net of interest income of $73,000) in 2004 from $714,000 (net of interest income of $15,000) in 2003.&#160; The decrease was attributable to lower average borrowings in addition to an increase in interest income.&#160; The weighted average interest rate on total debt was 3.6% in 2004 and 3.0% in 2003.</font></p>
<p>
<font size="2" face="Times New Roman">Income Taxes</font></p>
<p>
<font size="2" face="Times New Roman">The effective income tax rate was 38.0% in both 2004 and 2003.&#160; The effective income tax rate for both years differed from the statutory rate due primarily to state and local income taxes, net of the federal tax benefit.&#160; In 2005, increases in state income taxes are expected to increase the effective income tax rate to between 38.5% and 39.0%.</font></p>
<p>
<font size="2" face="Times New Roman"><b>2003 Compared to 2002</b></font></p>
<p>
<font size="2" face="Times New Roman">Net Sales</font></p>
<p>
<font size="2" face="Times New Roman">Net sales increased $38.2 million to $557.9 million in 2003, a 7.4% increase over net sales of $519.7 million in 2002.&#160; The increase was attributable to the sales generated by the 37 stores opened in 2003 and the effect of a full year&#146;s worth of sales for the 25 stores opened in 2002, partially offset by the closing of seven stores and a comparable store sales decrease of 3.0%.&#160; While sales of athletic footwear in our comparable stores increased slightly, our men&#146;s, women&#146;s and children&#146;s non-athletic footwear categories, along with our accessories, each experienced declines in comparable store sales.</font></p>
<p>
<font size="2" face="Times New Roman">Gross Profit</font></p>
<p>
<font size="2" face="Times New Roman">Gross profit increased $8.7 million to $158.2 million in 2003, a 5.8% increase from gross profit of $149.5 million in 2002.&#160; Our gross profit margin decreased to 28.4% from 28.8% in 2002.&#160; As a percentage of sales, the merchandise margin increased 0.2% and buying, distribution and occupancy costs increased 0.6%.&#160; The increase in the merchandise margin was primarily driven by increases in our adult athletic category and our children&#146;s category.&#160; The increase in buying, distribution and occupancy costs was primarily a result of the general deleveraging effect of the negative comparable store sales. </font></p>

<p><font size="2" face="Times New Roman">Selling, General and Administrative Expenses</font></p>
<p>
<font size="2" face="Times New Roman">Selling, general and administrative expenses increased $14.5 million to $138.2 million in 2003 from $123.7 million in 2002.&#160; As a percentage of sales, these expenses increased 1.0% in 2003 primarily due to the general deleveraging effect of the negative comparable store sales.&#160; During fiscal 2003, we incurred $789,000 in asset retirement costs on store closings, relocations and remodels in addition to impairment costs as compared to $278,000 in fiscal 2002.&#160; The aggregate of pre-opening expenses for the 37 new stores in 2003 was approximately $2.5 million, or 0.4% of sales, and was $2.0 million, or 0.4% of sales, for the 25 new stores in 2002.</font></p>
<p  align="center">
<font size="2" face="Times New Roman">18</font></p>



<div style="page-break-before:always"></div>
<PAGE>
<br>



<p>
<font size="2" face="Times New Roman">Interest Expense</font></p>
<p>
<font size="2" face="Times New Roman">Interest expense decreased to $714,000 (net of interest income of $15,000) in 2003 from $785,000 (net of interest income of $35,000) in 2002.&#160; The decrease was attributable to a lower effective interest rate partially offset by an increase in average borrowings outstanding. The weighted average interest rate on total debt was 3.0% in 2003 and 4.1% in 2002.</font></p>
<p>
<font size="2" face="Times New Roman">Income Taxes</font></p>
<p>
<font size="2" face="Times New Roman">The effective income tax rate was 38.0% in 2003 and 37.5% in 2002.&#160; The effective income tax rate for both years differed from the statutory rate due primarily to state and local income taxes, net of the federal tax benefit.&#160; The increase in the effective income tax rate in 2003 was due to higher state income taxes.</font></p>
<p>
